  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_02557604-14b5-4bad-a473-82befb45d3cd'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <span id="freeText8691829075651152554" style="display:none">Ensign Andrew Dahl has just been assigned to the Universal Union Capital Ship <i>Intrepid</i>, flagship of the Universal Union since the year 2456. It’s a prestige posting, and Andrew is thrilled all the more to be assigned to the ship’s Xenobiology laboratory.<br /><br />Life couldn’t be better…until Andrew begins to pick up on the fact that: <br />(1) every Away Mission involves some kind of lethal confrontation with alien forces <br />(2) the ship’s captain, its chief science officer, and the handsome Lieutenant Kerensky always survive these confrontations <br />(3) at least one low-ranked crew member is, sadly, always killed.<br /><br />Not surprisingly, a great deal of energy below decks is expended on avoiding, at all costs, being assigned to an Away Mission. Then Andrew stumbles on information that completely transforms his and his colleagues’ understanding of what the starship <i>Intrepid</i> really is…and offers them a crazy, high-risk chance to save their own lives.</span>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_15517815').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_15517815').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_15517815').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This question contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a href="/questions/6594-quick-question---and-its-coming-from-a"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Quick question - and its coming from a religious Jewish point of view. Towards the end of the story Hester is happy that at 120 years he can slip and break his neck and it will be his own fault. Why 120 years? According to the Jewish religion man (and woman for that matter ) can only live for 20 years, That's because 120 is how long Moses lived. And *no one* can live longer that Moses. Is that the reference? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>
</span>
</div>

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Could be, the book of Genesis before the story of Noah says: "So the LORD said: “My Spirit will not contend with man forever, for he is mortal; his da<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Could be, the book of Genesis before the story of Noah says: "So the LORD said: “My Spirit will not contend with man forever, for he is mortal; his days shall be 120 years.” - Genesis 6:3<br /><br />After that men stop living as long and fairly quickly arrive at living about 120 years.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div id="review_272264902" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/272264902" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/922495-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/272264902?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/922495-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer272264902" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer23461004744371055"><br/>Simply said, I loved this book. <br/><br/>If you want me to go into more detail, I can. But that's the heart of the matter.<br/><br/>I don't do spoilers. So instead, let me say this: This book was fun. It was clever. It was geeky. <br/><br/>But most of all, it made me laugh. I'm not just saying it was funny. I'm saying I actually laughed out loud in the restaurant where I was reading it. Then later at the coffee shop. Then later still when I was reading it at home, even though I was alone in the house. Even though that's wha</span>
  <span id="freeText23461004744371055" style="display:none"><br />Simply said, I loved this book. <br /><br />If you want me to go into more detail, I can. But that's the heart of the matter.<br /><br />I don't do spoilers. So instead, let me say this: This book was fun. It was clever. It was geeky. <br /><br />But most of all, it made me laugh. I'm not just saying it was funny. I'm saying I actually laughed out loud in the restaurant where I was reading it. Then later at the coffee shop. Then later still when I was reading it at home, even though I was alone in the house. Even though that's what crazy people do. <br /><br />I can honestly say I can't think of another book that ever made me laugh this much. Ever. <br /><br />So yeah. It's good stuff. A+. <br /><br />My one problem with this book is that it makes me feel a little threatened. I know John Scalzi. I like him. We're both successful authors, and we both live very happily in our respective corners of the sky. <br /><br />He writes snappy, clever sci-fi with good plot and action. I write huge tangled metafictional fantasy thingers. This is a good thing. It means that while we're neighbors, we're somewhat distant neighbors. This makes it easier for us to be friends, because we're both doing our own thing on our own ends of the street, metaphorically speaking.<br /><br />But now Scalzi has gone all meta. That's my turf. If this book wasn't so entertaining, I'd want to send some big, broad-shouldered men around to his house to look menacing and say things like, "Mr Rothfuss is worried about some of the writing you have been doing in his neighborhood. When Mr. Rothfuss gets worried about things, sometimes people get mysteriously hurt. With an axe handle." <br /><br />So yeah. Thanks for the early look at the book, Scalzi. Thanks for the vastly entertaining read. <br /><br />Just remember. Axe handle. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="23461004744371055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_367156717" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/367156717" />
    <a title="unknown" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2863914-unknown"><img alt="unknown" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579703867p2/2863914.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/367156717?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="unknown" class="user" itemprop="url" name="unknown" href="/user/show/2863914-unknown">unknown</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=sci-fi-fantasy">sci-fi-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=wssfbc">wssfbc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=meta">meta</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer367156717" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10639231049828558385">I was going to write a review for this one, but then I realized I could basically just cut and paste my review of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88072.Agent_to_the_Stars' title='Agent to the Stars by John Scalzi' rel='nofollow noopener'>Agent to the Stars</a>, changing relevant details like "plot" and "character names" and just keeping everything else exactly the same. Because this book, like all of Scalzi's books except maybe Old Man's War and the two Old Man's War books I didn't read because I didn't really like the second book, suffers (or maybe benefits, I don't know, apparently people <i>love</i> this dude) from being wri</span>
  <span id="freeText10639231049828558385" style="display:none">I was going to write a review for this one, but then I realized I could basically just cut and paste my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88072.Agent_to_the_Stars" title="Agent to the Stars by John Scalzi" rel="nofollow noopener">Agent to the Stars</a>, changing relevant details like "plot" and "character names" and just keeping everything else exactly the same. Because this book, like all of Scalzi's books except maybe Old Man's War and the two Old Man's War books I didn't read because I didn't really like the second book, suffers (or maybe benefits, I don't know, apparently people <i>love</i> this dude) from being written by John Scalzi. More specifically, John Scalzi, Popular Blogger and Honorary President of Internet Nerds. <br /><br />The plusses of being a book written by John Scalzi: you probably have a good premise, you can be read in about three hours, and you are probably going to sell pretty well. The negatives: you won't be as funny as you think you are, the cleverness of your premise will be worn thin even at a three-hour reading length, and all of your characters will sound exactly the same as not only each other, but all of the characters in <i>all</i> of John Scalzi's novels (more specifically, they will sound like a blog post by John Scalzi). Oh, also on the downside, Wil Wheaton will narrate you as an audiobook, which is bad news not only because you don't work very well as an audiobook (unless your listeners like it when every single line of dialogue is attributed, even during long, quippy back-and-forths, though I guess you could argue it is necessary since everyone sounds the same), but because Wil Wheaton is kind of shockingly terrible as a reader of audiobooks (though he <i>does</i> make all the characters sound the same, so maybe that's intentionally meta).<br /><br />Speaking of meta: this book is very in love with itself, especially the second half, and especially especially the three codas, which are kind of infuriating, if only because they reveal how lazy of a writer Scalzi is: he hints that there is this deeper level beyond his story, one worth exploring, even though he didn't bother writing a story interesting enough to make me care to do it.<br /><br />Because Scalzi is kind of lazy. I mean, the incredibly hard-working kind of lazy, where you get everything done, but you get it done <i>just enough</i>, and you think it doesn't matter because your boss didn't notice, or your teacher doesn't grade very hard and you got an A anyway, or you are a really nice guy and your fans are going to praise everything you do regardless of quality (hi, Joss Whedon!).<br /><br />I mean, consider: all the problems in this book, from the samey characters to the limp plot to the unearned sentimentality to the not-very-funny funny dialogue, could probably have been cleared up with, I don't know, another draft or two. But Scalzi is <i>very busy</i> being Scalzi, so he wrote this one in <a href="http://www.tor.com/blogs/2012/07/how-books-can-become-batman-scalzi-at-sdcc" rel="nofollow noopener">five weeks</a> (because NaNoWriMo just isn't enough time to do a thorough job), sent it off to his editor, and proceded to write 11 blogs posts and attend five comic book conventions before finally dropping into bed, exhausted, but confident that his teacher was probably still going to give him that A, because he's a really nice guy and always makes funny comments in class.<br /><br />And, judging by his NYT best-seller status, he got that A! But not from me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10639231049828558385" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17531485390809661131" style="display:none">4.5 stars<br><br><b>Re-read 2017</b><br>My original feeling for the book stand, but I did find the 3 Codas at the end seemed a <i>tad</i> more tedious and unneeded this time around. <br><br><b>Orignal review: 2015</b><br><br><i>
  <b>Fanfuckingtastic!</b>
</i> <br>I could totally slap myself for not reading this sooner.<br><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1424036472ra/13706968.gif" width="250" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The premise of this story is that Andy Dahl and a group of his friends, who all work aboard the starship Intrepid, start to notice a troubling pattern happening on the Away Missions.<br>And they aren't the only ones who've noticed...<br><br>Does everyone know what a <i>
  <b>Redshirt</b>
</i> is?<br>On the off-chance you aren't a Star Trek fan or maybe haven't been exposed to the term before, let me explain where it came from.<br>On every mission, Captain Kirk, Spock, Bones, etc. would get beamed to some uncharted planet to discover...<i>something</i>. But, of course, our main characters never beamed anywhere by themselves. <br>Not <i>really</i> .<br>They always took Ensign So-n-So (wearing his <b>red shirt</b>) with them. <br>And, inevitably, Ensign Redshirt ended up as cannon fodder.<br><br><b>Death by exploding rock!</b><br><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1424039439ra/13707448.gif" width="200" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So, after watching the show for any length of time, you knew what was going to eventually happen to anyone who happened to be wearing the wrong color in an uncharted area of space.<br><br><b>Death by zappy-beam!</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1424040218ra/13707528.gif" width="250" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Which is <i>hilarious</i> when you're watching re-runs and playing a drinking game!<br>But maybe it wouldn't quite so funny if the Redshirts were real characters?<br>Although, I laughed my ass off at Andy and his friends the entire time I was reading this, so that blows that theory right out of the water.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1424043534ra/13707900.gif" width="250" height="220" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I wasn't all that surprised that I thought this was funny, but I was really surprised that I was totally invested in the plot. And there <i>is</i> a plot! A really good one!<br>And the ENDING! <br>I did not see that twist coming, Mr. Scalzi. Well done, sir!<br>Alright, so what happens is...<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[hahahahahahahaha. No. I'm not telling you anything.<br>Go get this book if you wanna find out what makes this story so special. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1424045597ra/13708086.gif" width="250" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_1092316870" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1092316870" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1092316870?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1092316870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6118171979431093812">March 2020 reread, just because. It's surprising how much of this I forgot since I first read it 5 years ago ... and how much more I liked it this second time around. I loved all the meta stuff; it's pretty mindblowing. And I'm actually very fond of the three codas that make up the last quarter of the book. (I got a good chuckle when I realized that the three codas - First Person, Second Person and Third Person - are about three different minor characters from the main storyline, and the codas a</span>
  <span id="freeText6118171979431093812" style="display:none">March 2020 reread, just because. It's surprising how much of this I forgot since I first read it 5 years ago ... and how much more I liked it this second time around. I loved all the meta stuff; it's pretty mindblowing. And I'm actually very fond of the three codas that make up the last quarter of the book. (I got a good chuckle when I realized that the three codas - First Person, Second Person and Third Person - are about three different minor characters from the main storyline, and the codas are written in ... wait for it ... first person, second person and third person. How did I miss that the first time through? :D<br><br>4.5 stars!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1423107098i/13588319.jpg" width="250" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>What if the redshirt <s>extras</s> low-ranking crew members on <i><s>Star Trek</s> The Chronicles of the Intrepid</i> realized that whenever one of them accompanies the starship's officers on an away mission to a planet or somewhere, that crew member was extremely likely to be killed?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1423118653i/13589322.jpg" width="400" height="260" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Naturally, this creates a dog-eat-dog situation where the long-term members of the crew learn to disappear quickly whenever an officer comes around, and the newer members have a very high mortality rate. Finally, a few of the junior crew members decide to try to get to the bottom of this mysterious phenomenon and, if possible, try to find a way to end it.<br><br>This book is extremely funny in parts, especially if you're familiar with the original <i>Star Trek</i> and some of its characters and quirks, but it's really kind of an odd book at the same time. Most of the book is a satire, a little on the superficial side and very snappy-dialogue-driven. There's a lot to make fun of with <i>Trek</i>, as fond as I am of it, and it's not all about the callous and weird ways in which random crew members die. (I did think the captain here needed a few sexy alien ladies slinking up to him.) The answer to the mystery of why so many crew members are dying on this particular starship doesn't really hold water logically AT ALL, but I've suspended disbelief on a lot shakier plot lines in my Trek-watching over the years, and I was willing to roll with it.<br><br>Then the story gets a little bit more screwy and a lot more meta, and at about the 70% mark the main story ends. The rest of the book is three "codas" written from the points of view of three very minor characters from the first part of the story, telling a little more about what happened in their lives after the main story ended. They're interesting, but so very different in tone from the rest of the book that the contrast is a little jarring. They're kind of sobering, in fact. And no, that wasn't me you saw surreptitiously wiping away a tear as I turned the last page.<br><br>3 1/2 stars. I may decide to round up when I see how I feel about this in another day or two. ETA: I have in fact decided to round up, after chatting with Anne in the thread to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1123794064?comment=114694033#comment_114694033" rel="nofollow noopener">her very funny review</a>. It made me realize that I recall this book with a lot of affection, despite its flaws.<br><br>Content advisory: fairly frequent F-bombs, and some innuendos and crude talk in a few places.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6118171979431093812" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_346391758" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/346391758" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/346391758?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781429963602" href="/book/show/13538492-redshirts">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2014-reads">2014-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=hugo-nebula">hugo-nebula</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=locus-winner">locus-winner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2020-reads">2020-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer346391758" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7668160307326321303">2020 reread: still so much fun.<br/><blockquote>
  <i>“Sooner or later the Narrative will come for each of us.” </i>
</blockquote>Thanks to cultural osmosis even I, the person who (according to a colleague of mine) lost quite a few nerd points for having never seen a single <i>Star Trek</i> episode, am somehow familiar with the concept of 'redshirts' - the unfortunate extras whose lot is to die dramatically on screen just to pad the plot and remind the viewer that the near-invincible main characters are indeed, so to say, in a real pickle</span>
  <span id="freeText7668160307326321303" style="display:none">2020 reread: still so much fun.<br><blockquote>
  <i>“Sooner or later the Narrative will come for each of us.” </i>
</blockquote>Thanks to cultural osmosis even I, the person who (according to a colleague of mine) lost quite a few nerd points for having never seen a single <i>Star Trek</i> episode, am somehow familiar with the concept of 'redshirts' - the unfortunate extras whose lot is to die dramatically on screen just to pad the plot and remind the viewer that the near-invincible main characters are indeed, so to say, in a real pickle.<blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>"In other words, crew deaths are a feature, not a bug." </b>
  </i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1405570197i/10416380._SX540_.jpg" width="330" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>And so the story goes: a bunch of new crew members on a starship 'Intrepid' discover that despite being to themselves very much real,<b> they are little but the doomed 'Redshirts' on the not-so-good <i>Star Trek</i> rip-off third-rate TV show.</b><blockquote>
  <i>“It’s messed up that the most rational explanation for what does go on in this ship is that a television show intrudes on our reality and warps it. But that’s not the worst thing about it.”<br><br>“Jesus Christ,” Finn said. “If that’s not the worst thing, what is?”<br><br>“That as far as I can tell,” Jenkins said, “it’s not actually a very good show.”</i>
</blockquote>When the bizarre and incomprehensible to any real reason logic of TV 'Narrative' seizes control of their lives, the events and actions quickly turn rather cinematically idiotic, and a few crew members are invariably destined to die gruesome and pointless deaths while a few officers - clearly the show's protagonists - survive despite all odds. Over the years, certain crew members have even learned to semi-successfully game the system in order to survive. <b>Because what else can you do but succumb to the Narrative, no matter how poorly written and unfair it is?</b><blockquote>
  <i>“We've already established whoever is writing us is an asshole.”</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1405570197i/10416381._SX540_.jpg" width="330" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>This was my first time reading anything by John Scalzi, and it kept me quite entertained, assuring that it will not be the last. There was something about its constantly shifting style and message that suits my easily distractible nature - I guess it was the constant oscillation between smart satire, slapstick comedy, eye rolling 'meta', sappy sentimentality and unexpected infusion of searching for the meaning of life and self-discovery. All together it creates an interesting combination that at times flows smoothly and at times teeters on the verge of losing its footing just a bit. <br><br><b>But overall it worked. </b>It worked even despite the (honestly, quite unnecessary) three 'codas' stuck onto the ending if the book as though their only function was to pad the word count just a little bit. I wish they were left out, and the ending - just the way it was, abrupt and unexpected and yet completely necessary given the internal logic of the narrative - was left to stand on its own. (2020 edit: I did not mind the codas this time - go figure.)<br><br>But even with the extra padding, the story worked.<blockquote>
  <i>“Whether you're an extra or the hero, this story is about to end. When it's done, whatever you want to be will be up to you and only you. It will happen away from the eyes of any audience and from the hand of any writer. You will be your own man.”</i>
</blockquote>I liked it, I really did. I had fun reading it, and now I'm trying to decide which Scalzi book I want to try next. Thoughts?<blockquote>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1405570197i/10416382.jpg" width="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>And no, I'll never ever again will be able to look at any TV show without imagining the implications of the script governing the lives of real people.<blockquote>
  <i>“Other science fiction shows had science advisers and consultants," Hanson pointed out.<br><br>"It's science fiction," Weinstein said. "The second part of that phrase matters too."<br><br>"But you're making it bad science fiction," Hester said. "And we have to live in it.”</i>
</blockquote><br>4 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7668160307326321303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1009642677" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1009642677" />
    <a title="Alejandro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro"><img alt="Alejandro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328672389p2/665584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1009642677?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alejandro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alejandro" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro">Alejandro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780765334794" href="/book/show/15793281-redshirts">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=star-trek">star-trek</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=alternate-reality">alternate-reality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1009642677" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9730362364010193475"><b>
  <u>Death... the Final Fate...<br/>These are the voyages of the <i>Starship Intrepid</i>.<br/>Its continuing mission,<br/>to kill naive new crewmembers,<br/>to seek out cheap thrills and easy lamentations,<br/>to boldly go where no redshirt has gone before!</u>
</b><br/><br/>I knew since some time ago about this novel but until now I had been able to read it, and I am truly glad that I did it!<br/><br/>One of the best <i>Star Trek</i> novels without really being about <i>Star Trek</i>.<br/><br/>It's a common knowledge but again for those neophytes to the topic, let me explain th</span>
  <span id="freeText9730362364010193475" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>Death... the Final Fate...<br />These are the voyages of the <i>Starship Intrepid</i>.<br />Its continuing mission,<br />to kill naive new crewmembers,<br />to seek out cheap thrills and easy lamentations,<br />to boldly go where no redshirt has gone before!</u>
</b><br /><br />I knew since some time ago about this novel but until now I had been able to read it, and I am truly glad that I did it!<br /><br />One of the best <i>Star Trek</i> novels without really being about <i>Star Trek</i>.<br /><br />It's a common knowledge but again for those neophytes to the topic, let me explain that "Red Shirt" is a term that people started to use refering for those poor crewmembers, sometimes even without a proper name, accompanying the main officers of the <i>Starship Enterprise</i>, in the Original Series of <i>Star Trek</i>. Usually they wear "red shirts" due that Security Officers wear uniforms with shirts colored in red. And to add a feeling of "danger and realism" to the series, the scriptwriters decided to kill some low level crewmember in each away mission while visiting some dangerous planet or some mysterious space station.<br /><br />This recurring gag became so common in the Original Series that the term of "Red Shirt" or "Redshirt" is usually apply to any "extra" killed in any TV series and/or film to obtain thrills from the audience.<br /><br />John Scalzi, the author, came up with this brilliant story merging smart humor, nasty dangers and wonderful science fiction, consolidating a great novel, obviously appealing to any Trekker, but also to any reader interested in science fiction in general or even looking for an amusing book.<br /><br />Without a doubt, one of the most polemic topics in any story is when a character is killed off the <i>narrative</i>. People may think that killing a secondary or even tertiary character cannot do a negative effect to the audience, but one has to think about that for some people may be a non-important character but it may be relevant even the reason of why the reader is keeping up with the story. Also, giving less value to the "life" of a character just because he/she wasn't important on the story, in a way, is like provoking an effect of not value a life if they haven't acomplished something worthy of mention in history.<br /><br />Killing characters always is controversial.<br /><br />If they became extremely popular, it can't alienate audience to keep up reading/watching any series. Also, it's sad to think about all the lost potential for that character since he/she is no longer in the story. Even worse if one realizes that he/she was killed off just to avoid to became a character more popular than certain other key character that the writer may appreciate more. But definitely, the worst of all is when a character is introduced, giving him/her a lot of developing but is killed off before of acomplishing anything of value to the story.<br /><br />And of course, we have the issue of returning back from the dead. That happens when the characters are too important to keep them dead and/or to make an event about the resurrection of the characters. That certainly has its positive and negative repercussions.<br /><br />Getting back to the novel, while it's basic premise is to exploit the recurring gag of killing off characters to get cheap thrills, also this excellent book is a great example of a crafty story with priceless science fiction and fantasy.<br /><br />The only "bad" thing that I can think of is a section once the story ends, with three "codas" telling like side-stories about some characters whom appeared on the book. Honestly, I didn't need that, it was like anti-climatic having finished the novel and then finding those "codas", that they didn't give me any info of importante to change my impression of the novel. I don't know if they were included only to reach a 300-page level, that in previous times I have commented that if a story is good, it's doesn't need lot of pages to be seen as a worthy material. But again, if you opted of not reading those additional "codas" to the book, well, don't do it. You won't miss much.<br /><br />However, the novel is indeed a fine masterpiece of crafty reading.<br /><br />Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9730362364010193475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_305210735" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/305210735" />
    <a title="Kemper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/405390-kemper"><img alt="Kemper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495476851p2/405390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/305210735?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kemper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kemper" href="/user/show/405390-kemper">Kemper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=tv-movies">tv-movies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer305210735" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer908214046410885003">Halfway through this book, I was thinking that I could do something funny in this review with Will Farrell’s character from the movie <i>Stranger Than Fiction</i>. But then it had to go and actually reference <i>Stranger Than Fiction</i> and ruin my plans.<br/><br/>Thanks for nothing, Scalzi!<br/><br/>Anyhow, in the distant future the starship <i>Intrepid</i> explores<br/>the galaxy with a crew led by a bold captain and his logical science officer. A group of newly assigned crew members quickly notice that odd and bizarre things happen when</span>
  <span id="freeText908214046410885003" style="display:none">Halfway through this book, I was thinking that I could do something funny in this review with Will Farrell’s character from the movie <i>Stranger Than Fiction</i>. But then it had to go and actually reference <i>Stranger Than Fiction</i> and ruin my plans.<br /><br />Thanks for nothing, Scalzi!<br /><br />Anyhow, in the distant future the starship <i>Intrepid</i> explores<br />the galaxy with a crew led by a bold captain and his logical science officer. A group of newly assigned crew members quickly notice that odd and bizarre things happen when they get around the captain and his senior staff. Routine missions become dangerous. The basic laws of physics seem to break down. Common sense is forgotten. And god help you if you’re one of the unlucky bastards who ends up on an away team with them because you’re probably coming back to the ship in a body bag. Assuming there’s enough left of your corpse to bring back at all.<br /><br />Scalzi took what could be a one-joke premise from the old gag about the extras wearing red on <i>Star Trek</i> being disposable and having a very short shelf life, and he turned that concept into a funny and interesting examination on the nature of fiction and free will. I don’t want to say too much about the plot because the twists and turns are part of the fun, but I liked how the book went off in a couple of surprising directions.<br /><br />While I had fun with it, Scalzi’s style is becoming a bit odd to me, and that was enough to take it from four stars to three. He skips almost any kind of descriptive writing to focus almost exclusively on dialogue driven plot. For example, there isn’t a single word about what the ship or the characters look like. Skimping on the exposition in the interest of rushing through the main part of the story makes it compulsively readable, but it also makes it feel a bit thin. So while I was completely into the story of the crew fighting against their fate, a few more details about what that looked like would have been welcome.<br /><br />One other minor nitpick. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ If television hasn’t existed for centuries, how are all of the crew instantly aware of things like commercial breaks and the tricks routinely used to heighten the drama? I guess in light of the conversation near the end before the codas, you could make a case that this is part of the even larger narrative, but it was strange. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Despite some of my problems with it, Scalzi still writes with heart and humor, and sci-fi could use a lot more of that.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="908214046410885003" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_774883686" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/774883686" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/774883686?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer774883686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17352187620848743503">Redshirts: A Novel with Three Codas by John “I’m not Gene Roddenberry but I dressed up like him once for Halloween” Scalzi could have also been Redshirts: Existential Musings in the Internet Age by a Satire Minded Child of the Eighties. <br/><br/>Winner of the Hugo and the Locus Award, I can see the spinning wheels in the minds of the award voters / selection committee of both trophies – “A fun, unique twist on an old Star Trek gag but then he goes and ends it with an existential quest for meaning amidst </span>
  <span id="freeText17352187620848743503" style="display:none">Redshirts: A Novel with Three Codas by John “I’m not Gene Roddenberry but I dressed up like him once for Halloween” Scalzi could have also been Redshirts: Existential Musings in the Internet Age by a Satire Minded Child of the Eighties. <br><br>Winner of the Hugo and the Locus Award, I can see the spinning wheels in the minds of the award voters / selection committee of both trophies – “A fun, unique twist on an old Star Trek gag but then he goes and ends it with an existential quest for meaning amidst spiritual ennui and isolation with a timely cultural message and pulls a satisfying ending out of the hat.” <br><br>No kidding.<br><br>I think it was the three codas, told from first, second and third person perspectives that put this over the top, the element in this book that lifted it deep over the centerfielder’s head and into the bleachers. As Jeff Lebowski would say “it really tied the room together.”<br><br>A fun mix of Galaxy Quest, Weird Science, Stranger than Fiction, and Heinlein’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50877.The_Number_of_the_Beast__The_World_As_Myth_" title="The Number of the Beast (The World As Myth) by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">The Number of the Beast</a>, Redshirts is funny, entertaining, quirky, baffling, mind bending and thought provoking and even, yes, touching. There are some gaps in the narrative, some stretches in the already strained science, and some oddly thin characterizations, but all the same this is a winner.<br><br>One recurring theme that I notice in Scalzi’s work is a playful satire/ tribute of sci-fi classics: Redshirts to Star Trek, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51964.Old_Man_s_War__Old_Man_s_War___1_" title="Old Man's War (Old Man's War, #1) by John Scalzi" rel="nofollow noopener">Old Man's War</a> to Heinlein, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9647532.Fuzzy_Nation" title="Fuzzy Nation by John Scalzi" rel="nofollow noopener">Fuzzy Nation</a> to Little Fuzzy and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7081.The_Android_s_Dream" title="The Android's Dream by John Scalzi" rel="nofollow noopener">The Android's Dream</a> to Philip K. Dick. <br><br>I like what he’s done with the place, Live Long and Prosper!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1508626507i/24237066.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="17352187620848743503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=funny">funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=the-scalz">the-scalz</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer423787463" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2694128292449578444">The starship Intrepid seeks out new worlds and boldly goes where no man has gone before. However, as Ensign Andrew Dahl soon discovers, low-ranked crew members die more often aboard the Intrepid than brain cells at a Spring Break weekend while the senior officers, besides Lt. Kerensky, always survive without a scratch. As they dig deeper, what will Andy and his friends uncover?<br/><br/>Anyone who's watched more than two or three episodes of Star Trek knows that it's always the extra, or redshirt, that di</span>
  <span id="freeText2694128292449578444" style="display:none">The starship Intrepid seeks out new worlds and boldly goes where no man has gone before. However, as Ensign Andrew Dahl soon discovers, low-ranked crew members die more often aboard the Intrepid than brain cells at a Spring Break weekend while the senior officers, besides Lt. Kerensky, always survive without a scratch. As they dig deeper, what will Andy and his friends uncover?<br /><br />Anyone who's watched more than two or three episodes of Star Trek knows that it's always the extra, or redshirt, that dies when the crew beams down to a planet or any other location that's not on the bridge of the ship. Why is that? That's the question Redshirts poses to the reader.<br /><br />This is not my favorite book by John Scalzi. It's not even in the top three. I love the Scalz and his brand of wit. Too bad this one was all wit and very little shi... substance. Andy and his friends were an interesting bunch. I liked how they gradually pieced things together. Wait... no they didn't. It was pretty much all handed to them. <br /><br />The writing actually seemed a little on the lazy side. It was mostly dialogue and very little description. I had no idea how any of the characters looked or even what the interior of the Intrepid was supposed to look like.<br /><br />The story is meant to be a takeoff of Star Trek but it felt more like several episodes of Red Dwarf, most recently the Back to Earth movie where the crew arrived on earth and encountered the actors that played them. I wasn't a tremendous fan of that one either.<br /><br />Still, Redshirts had its humorous moments and the absurd logic was consistent. It just wasn't very substantial and I thought it wore a little thin toward the end. I actually enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9647532.Fuzzy_Nation" title="Fuzzy Nation by John Scalzi" rel="nofollow noopener">Fuzzy Nation</a> more. It's a low three and I could have safely missed it. If you're wanting to read John Scalzi, skip this one and pick up <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51964.Old_Man_s_War__Old_Man_s_War___1_" title="Old Man's War (Old Man's War, #1) by John Scalzi" rel="nofollow noopener">Old Man's War</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2694128292449578444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_350691578" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/350691578" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/350691578?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer350691578" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9460323646381805706"><a href='http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=xQIuapbeh0I' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Theme Song of the Book!</a> Any song whose chorus begins with "They said this air would be breathable" gets my vote.<br/><br/>Rating: 3.5* of five <br/><br/><b>The Publisher Says</b>: Ensign Andrew Dahl has just been assigned to the Universal Union Capital Ship "Intrepid," flagship of the Universal Union since the year 2456. It's a prestige posting, and Andrew is thrilled all the more to be assigned to the ship's Xenobiology laboratory. Life couldn't be better...until Andrew begins to pick up on the fact that (1) every Aw</span>
  <span id="freeText9460323646381805706" style="display:none"><a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=xQIuapbeh0I" rel="nofollow noopener">The Theme Song of the Book!</a> Any song whose chorus begins with "They said this air would be breathable" gets my vote.<br /><br />Rating: 3.5* of five <br /><br /><b>The Publisher Says</b>: Ensign Andrew Dahl has just been assigned to the Universal Union Capital Ship "Intrepid," flagship of the Universal Union since the year 2456. It's a prestige posting, and Andrew is thrilled all the more to be assigned to the ship's Xenobiology laboratory. Life couldn't be better...until Andrew begins to pick up on the fact that (1) every Away Mission involves some kind of lethal confrontation with alien forces, (2) the ship's captain, its chief science officer, and the handsome Lieutenant Kerensky always survive these confrontations, and (3) at least one low-ranked crew member is, sadly, always killed.<br /><br />Not surprisingly, a great deal of energy below decks is expended on avoiding, at all costs, being assigned to an Away Mission. Then Andrew stumbles on information that completely transforms his and his colleagues' understanding of what the starship "Intrepid "really is...and offers them a crazy, high-risk chance to save their own lives.<br /><br /><b>My Review</b>: Yeah, well, you know what? John Scalzi wrote a doozy here. Starship <i>Intrepid</i> and its crew are a steal from classic <i>Trek</i>, and the steal is a new basic cable show (is anyone at Paramount listening?) and one of my main complaints about reality is addressed: Why is it that the really good ones aren't real? Why, to take a recent example of my gritching, can't I apply for a visa and go to Islandia? (They wouldn't let me in, and if they did I'd kick up such a fuss about not wanting to leave that they'd let me stay, and that could get...interesting.)<br /><br />If the Universe is truly infinite, not just reallyreallyreally big, all of fiction is fact somewhere and somewhen. It can't not be. In Infinity, do the same laws of physics apply everywhere? Perhaps not. Infinity is mind-boggling even to grasp at the edges of, and there is no way my monkey brain with its paltry senses and trivial education can know any more than the merest shadow of what it means.<br /><br />So somewhere the <i>Enterprise</i> and the <i>Intrepid</i> cruise among the stars and James T. Kirk and Spock do the nasty and <i>Serenity</i> smuggles cattle from moon to moon...and I get a visa for Islandia.<br /><br />Think about the implications of that for your own life briefly: He *does change. She *doesn't leave. You *do have that baby, adopt that child, build that family—the one you work on in your daydreams.<br /><br />Mindfuck.<br /><br />Glorious. Or maybe not. Maybe this is the life where your loved one dies. Why you? Why do you get the shit end of the stick? Or pause for a moment and think loving thoughts to the you that had that accident, took that fall, bore that loss.<br /><br />Look, not one of us gets out of here alive. What we have, while we're here, are choices and opportunities. And no choice, no opportunity, comes without a cost. Someyou, somewhere, pays, and this book's scorpion sting is “now you know, so what ya gonna do?”<br /><br />Dunno what I myownself will do, but now, thanks to a funny, engaging, and still very serious novel, I won't do it unknowingly, blindly, without considering the resonances. And yet, for all the longface puritanism that sounds like I'm feeling, it's less hair shirt than rib-tickler that got me here.<br /><br />John Scalzi! Yo! Good one, little dude. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9460323646381805706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1472455476" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1472455476" />
    <a title="Robin (Bridge Four)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14105531-robin-bridge-four"><img alt="Robin (Bridge Four)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473392517p2/14105531.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1472455476?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin (Bridge Four)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin (Bridge Four)" href="/user/show/14105531-robin-bridge-four">Robin (Bridge Four)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14105531-robin-bridge-four?shelf=awesome-audio">awesome-audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14105531-robin-bridge-four?shelf=chick-i-want-to-hang-out-with">chick-i-want-to-hang-out-with</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14105531-robin-bridge-four?shelf=buddy-read">buddy-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14105531-robin-bridge-four?shelf=super-sidekicks">super-sidekicks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14105531-robin-bridge-four?shelf=4-5-stars">4-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14105531-robin-bridge-four?shelf=fantastic-fantasy">fantastic-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14105531-robin-bridge-four?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1472455476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2099281983827130588"><b>John Scalzi is the Amazon daily deal today 15Aug20</b> <a href='https://www.amazon.com/dp/B0079XPUOW/ref=s9_acsd_al_bw_c2_x_13_t?pf_rd_m=ATVPDKIKX0DER&pf_rd_s=merchandised-search-2&pf_rd_r=W7DQYT6WHBRFA5J55AD7&pf_rd_t=101&pf_rd_p=0c1f5c3c-ef86-4e78-856f-fce7ca59e5ac&pf_rd_i=11552285011' rel='nofollow noopener'>$2.99</a> I really liked this one.<br/><br/>If you liked Star Trek and Galaxy Quest it might be right up your alley.<br/><br/><b>4.5 Guy is Gonna Die Stars</b><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1450887581ra/17481331.gif' width='400' height='170' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Buddy Read with my Sci-Fi loving peeps at <a>Buddies Books and Baubles</a><br/><br/>RedShirts seems a lot like a combo of Galaxy Quest and Stranger Than Fiction which means <b>I LOVED IT!!!</b> This hit all my Sci-Fi fangirl hot buttons making fun of all the tropes from those early Sci-Fi t.v. shows that didn’t realize were happening back when</span>
  <span id="freeText2099281983827130588" style="display:none"><b>John Scalzi is the Amazon daily deal today 15Aug20</b> <a href="https://www.amazon.com/dp/B0079XPUOW/ref=s9_acsd_al_bw_c2_x_13_t?pf_rd_m=ATVPDKIKX0DER&amp;pf_rd_s=merchandised-search-2&amp;pf_rd_r=W7DQYT6WHBRFA5J55AD7&amp;pf_rd_t=101&amp;pf_rd_p=0c1f5c3c-ef86-4e78-856f-fce7ca59e5ac&amp;pf_rd_i=11552285011" rel="nofollow noopener">$2.99</a> I really liked this one.<br><br>If you liked Star Trek and Galaxy Quest it might be right up your alley.<br><br><b>4.5 Guy is Gonna Die Stars</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1450887581ra/17481331.gif" width="400" height="170" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Buddy Read with my Sci-Fi loving peeps at <a>Buddies Books and Baubles</a><br><br>RedShirts seems a lot like a combo of Galaxy Quest and Stranger Than Fiction which means <b>I LOVED IT!!!</b> This hit all my Sci-Fi fangirl hot buttons making fun of all the tropes from those early Sci-Fi t.v. shows that didn’t realize were happening back when I grew up watching them.<br><br>I think MacGyver was the first show I loved from my childhood, that when I went back to it with my new adult eyes I saw all the problems with it and so we made a drinking game out of it. For every out of date piece of technology, MacGyvered solution and stock footage (a.k.a. every car case) you have to take a drink. I never made it through a single episode without getting drunk. I’m sure I could have played the same type of game with Star Trek for when Captain Kirk kisses an alien chick, an away team picks a random guy to go the planet with them, some science gets all screwy or someone shows up on the bridge when they could have just sent a message.<br><br>This book makes total fun of the fact that Redshirts or side crew are just fodder for the Sci-Fi killing machine to die in horrible and sometimes ridiculous ways.<br><blockquote>
  <i>“Ensign Davis thought, Screw this, I want to live, and swerved to avoid the land worms. But then he tripped and one of the land worms ate his face and he died anyway.”</i>
</blockquote><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1450887581ra/17481332.gif" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I loved the group of new cadets. They have a great chemistry and the banter between them is a lot of fun. As a girl I especially loved Duvall as she gets to break free of some of the normal gender roles that used to be assigned to woman in a Sci-Fi series <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[she actually gets to have a fling without much emotional attachment to it, because girls can do that too <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. But most of the story is shown to us through the eyes of Dahl who notices that things are not okay on this new ship he is on and people seem to be dying left and right on Away Teams. Also, what’s in the box???<br><blockquote>
  <i>"""What is it?"<br>"It's the Box."<br>"That's it? 'The Box'?"<br><b>"If it makes you feel better to think it's an experimental quantum-based computer with advanced inductive artificial intelligence capacity, whose design comes to us from an advanced but extinct race of warrior-engineers, then you can think about it that way."</b></i>
</blockquote><br>I had the best time as they all discover that something is not quite right with the people on the ship and why that might be. But even when you know what is going on how do you keep yourself from dying?<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450887812i/17481363._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="426" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It’s so sad but really true.<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450887812i/17481364.png" width="400" height="225" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>What is really happening and how do they fix it????</b> Well for that you will need to read the book….muwahhahaha. But if you are a sci-fi geek that loved things like Star Trek, Star Wars, Battlestar Galactica (the original), Buck Rogers etc. then this might be exactly what you need to scratch that itch. <br><br>After the main story there are 3 codas. The shift in the story is dramatic at this point. It is almost like picking out a slightly different book and I have no idea how to talk about them without spoiling so click at your own risk. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>Coda 1 – What do you do when you are a writer that learns all the things he has been writing are real and have created a world where he is responsible for almost everything that happens in it? I kinda liked seeing this perspective after the fact even though it is a huge shift from the tone of the original story. But the way the writer works through it was very interesting and funny at times.<br><blockquote><i>“Filled with existential ennui about your place in the universe? Get over yourself. Yes, you're an inconsequential worm in the grand scope of history. But you're an inconsequential worm who makes shit up for a living, which means that you don't have to lift heavy boxes or ask people if they want fries with that. Grow up and get back to work.” </i></blockquote>
<br>Coda 2 – Hester and the coma boy. Well it was interesting to see what happened to him after the fact it was a bit slow but interesting to see how the swap worked on the other end. <br><br>Coda 3 – This was probably my favorite Coda since it is a little bit of a romantic tale and the beginning of a love story. I really liked how it was presented and it actually made me feel worse for Jenkins. But my head got a little caught up in the quandary of it since the writer and Jenkins ended up being the dopplegangers. I wondered if he felt like his love life was that tragic and that is why Jenkins wife had to die or was that the kind of love he had always longed for and romanticized in his head. Or did it really have nothing to do with it other than chance. I did love the very end of this coda though as it was so full of possibility and hope. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="2099281983827130588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer3180478341126158127">💥 <b>Free until March 20</b> for members of Tor.com’s eBook of the Month Club! You can <b>sign up</b> (also for free) and <b>get the book</b> <a href='https://ebookclub.tor.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>!<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1554139579i/27300168._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>[<b>December 2015</b>]<br/><br/>· <b>BR with the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/148164-buddies-books-and-baubles' rel='nofollow noopener'>BBB gang</a></b> ·<br/><br/><b>Okay. Everyone says this book reminds them of Galaxy Quest</b>. And after saying this book reminds them of Galaxy Quest, said people usually start squeaking around in glee like manic mice. Well I have a confession to make: I am one of those freaks who hardly ever watches movies and/or TV. <i><b>Please don't break down and cry</b>. I know how diffi</i></span>
  <span id="freeText3180478341126158127" style="display:none">💥 <b>Free until March 20</b> for members of Tor.com’s eBook of the Month Club! You can <b>sign up</b> (also for free) and <b>get the book</b> <a href="https://ebookclub.tor.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1554139579i/27300168._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>[<b>December 2015</b>]<br><br>· <b>BR with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/148164-buddies-books-and-baubles" rel="nofollow noopener">BBB gang</a></b> ·<br><br><b>Okay. Everyone says this book reminds them of Galaxy Quest</b>. And after saying this book reminds them of Galaxy Quest, said people usually start squeaking around in glee like manic mice. Well I have a confession to make: I am one of those freaks who hardly ever watches movies and/or TV. <i><b>Please don't break down and cry</b>. I know how difficult it must be for you to accept I'm not as perfect as you thought I was, but it's the sad, sad truth. Fear not my friends, for as impossibly intolerable the situation might seem right now, in a few years time this will be naught but a bitter memory.</i> Hmm. I'm pretty sure there was a fascinating point to this paragraph. Can't remember now. Oh yes, <b>Galaxy Quest and the squeaking, manic mice</b>! So the fascinating point was this: I haven't seen Galaxy Quest, and have no idea what the manic mice are squeaking about. That's it. Bye now ← <i>now aren't you glad you read this paragraph? So informative! So captivating! I'm pretty sure it will change the history of reviews for all eternity.</i> And this is where I add a useless Galaxy Quest gif to my review for absolutely no reason. <b>You're welcome</b>.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451664681ra/17574059.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Anyway</b>, Freaking Galaxy Quest or not, the first part of this book was <b>nothing short of awesome</b>. Now. I kind of hate space stuff. <i>Yes, I've decided to crush both my utter perfection and stellar reputation to bits in this review. I sincerely hope you survive the sore disappointment.</i> So. <b>Space stuff pretty much bores me to death</b> (except from Star Wars. Obviously. And Star Trek. Obviously. And other non-boring space stuff. Obviously). But anyway. <b>I LOVED the first part of the book</b>. It was cool. It was funny. It was goofy. I loved that Scalzi used all the <b>Ancient Super Crappy Sci-Fi TV Tropes</b> (ASCSFTT™) as a base for his story. I obviously loved that the Redshirts (aka lowly crew members) kept dying in the most <b>creative, horrible, painfully hilarious way</b> (what can I say? I love bloodshed and atrocious agony). And I loved the <s>Star Trek</s> Intrepid crew. Oh, and I loved <b>The Box that Goes Ding</b>, too. Yeah, The Box was really cool. And there was that thing about owing people handjobs, blowjobs, and well, err, fucks. <b>Yeah, I really liked that, too</b>.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1398208167ra/9384828.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Ooops, I think we just shocked Kirky and friends</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Spocky doesn't look that shocked now, does he? Hmm, interesting <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. I can't say I'm surprised at their reaction, though. I mean, things like these were just not done, back in the days. And none of the Enterprise crew members would be caught dead engaging in such <b>garish, indecent proceedings</b>. Kirky wouldn't allow it. Besides, I'm pretty sure there were no such things as blowjobs, back in the 60s <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451551340i/17557664.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Oh dear, I guess I was wrong. On both accounts. <b>We really didn't need to know that about Kirky, did we?</b> *shudders* But hey, I did tell you there was something fishy going on with Spocky, so this shouldn't come as such a big surprise. Okay, better change subjects before we start getting filthy Star Trek images in our little heads, right? <b>Right</b>. <br><br><b>So anyway, this was me during the first part of the book</b>:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451551340ra/17557665.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>And this was me after the BIG TWIST</b>:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1450379943ra/17418176.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><b>Yeah, well, not really</b>. So the BIG TWIST happened and the book went FLOP. I could have been great. Except that it wasn't. It could have been exciting. Except that it wasn't. <b>It could have been as cool as the first part of the story. Except that it wasn't</b>. What it actually was? Tedious. And flat. And dull. And tedious. And flat. And dull. And NO I am not going to telll you about the BIG TWIST. Because spoilers. And stuff. Suffice it to say that <b>the space setting went POOF, and I went YAWN</b>. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451662767i/17573829.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Damn, to think I was actually enjoying space stuff for once</b>. To think I wasn't finding it tedious. And flat. And dull. John Scalzi, I don't like you very much right now. Why ruin a perfectly entertaining space stuff story by taking said space stuff out of said story? <b>Beats me</b>. Anyway, here I was, yawning and mourning the loss of the awesome space stuff, when all of a sudden <b>the story itself went POOF</b>. One page you're reading and wondering if anything is going to happen to wake you from your slumber, and next thing you know: <b>BOOM, the end</b>. At 70%. Freaking fantastic. Then the author proceeds to feed you three "codas," which are even more boring and uninteresting than the boring, uninteresting part of the actual story. Yay. <b>How positively exciting</b>. Woo hoo. I want more. Yesss. Amazing. <b>Okay</b>. I think that now would be a good time to end this review. If I don't, I might end up lowering my rating. And my opinion about this book is unpopular enough as it is. So I think I'll leave you in the company of Spocky, who is <b>not shocked by the idea of owing his fellow crew members blowjobs, and has a cool cat</b>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451662767i/17573830.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_2430194782" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2430194782" />
    <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton"><img alt="J.L.   Sutton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438142892p2/3675162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2430194782?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.L.   Sutton" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton">J.L.   Sutton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2430194782" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12799103846376193029"> In John Scalzi’s The Redshirts, the newest ensigns aboard the Union Capital Flagship, the Intrepid, recognize they are expendable. Not only that, they realize that every episodic confrontation of their ship requires that a low-ranking crew member (namely them) be killed. They do what they can to avoid the captain and the other bridge crew, but they know their days aboard the Intrepid are numbered. With a bit of research, they learn they are in a similar position to characters in a television sh</span>
  <span id="freeText12799103846376193029" style="display:none"> In John Scalzi’s The Redshirts, the newest ensigns aboard the Union Capital Flagship, the Intrepid, recognize they are expendable. Not only that, they realize that every episodic confrontation of their ship requires that a low-ranking crew member (namely them) be killed. They do what they can to avoid the captain and the other bridge crew, but they know their days aboard the Intrepid are numbered. With a bit of research, they learn they are in a similar position to characters in a television show, Star Trek, which aired about 200 years earlier. That knowledge doesn’t change their situation, though. The real question becomes: Can they assert control over their lives or does the unseen script completely control their destiny? <br /><br />Redshirts started out entertaining, but how far could Scalzi take the premise? As it turns out, quite a distance. Three codas worth of distance. Most of the material up to the first coda was enjoyable. That in itself is an accomplishment. I also thought the first coda was okay, but by the time I’d finished reading the last coda the life had gone out of the story. I was more than ready for it to be over. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12799103846376193029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
